Oh you, most dear sons, I have completed this book which is the last of the science of medicine along with its content, and I have made an end to it through its explanations and by its declarations. And it has come to me in a vision that I would complete this for you with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, namely "surgery".
And because the work of hands is forgotten in our region and is entirely estranged from everything else, so that the science has perished and been lost, and nothing remains of it except the small descriptions in books of the ancients which the hands have moved. And from this has come error and vanity, and the intentions are closed and the science is estranged.
Now it seems to me that I will revive it together with the ordering of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will also come together with the forms of cautery bandages and the other instruments of the work along with the adjustment of the declaration and preparation for it, as is necessary.
And because of this reason that no good masters of hands are found in our time, the art of medicine is estranged. And it is necessary for whoever undertakes this to be well-versed in the science of anatomy, as Galen says, so long as he knows the joints of the members, and their forms and conjunctions and separations, and has knowledge of the bones and nerves and blood vessels, and the number of them, and their movements and rest, and from where they issue.
And for this reason does Hippocrates say that a name with the name is much, and with the work is little; and similarly in the art of hand.
We warn you in the introduction to this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the things previously said, should beware lest they err and harm people; thus have I often warned many of those who undertake this science and abandon their knowledge and experience.
For I have seen a foolish surgeon who cut off i.e. amputated the neck of a woman with scrofula or a goiter, and he cut the arteries of the neck; why so much blood flowed out that the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I have seen another surgeon who treated a stone in the bladder of a man, and the stone was large; so he took with him a piece of the bladder, and the man died within three days.
And I had already been called to treat him, and I saw the condition of the sick man and the size of the stone, and knew well that if they were to remove it, he would die.
And I have seen another surgeon who took on the treatment of a duke from our land, who had his leg broken and with the break was also wounded. And the ignorant surgeon tightened the splint over the wound with a strong bandage, and did not leave any space for ointment, and left it thus until the end; and he commanded that it should not be loosened, and thus the duke's leg remained as it was, all inflamed and putrid, and came to ruin.
I was called to come to him, and I hastened to come to loosen the leg splint; and when I loosened it, he felt at ease and did not feel any pain.
But despite everything, the corruption was already confirmed in the limb and I could not heal it; and the corruption did not cease to progress through the member until the entire limb had been lost.
And I have seen another surgeon who opened up an infected abscess, and then inflamed it further through his malpractice.
For the chancre, when it is free of melancholic humour, one must not touch it with iron if it is not in a place where everything that is corrupted can be completely removed.
And therefore, most excellent son, it is necessary for you to know that manual work is divided into two parts: namely, the work where there is danger in several conditions; and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.
Why it is necessary for you to beware of it, so that foolish people cannot speak ill of you.
If you take up your arms in custody, and approach your illnesses lightly and firmly, and use the best method so that by the grace of God you come to salvation and are praised; and leave terrifying diseases with grievous sanitation; and remove your arms from those things that make you doubt, and have courage within yourself so that you can come into this world from another in glory.
The Gauls said in some of their warnings: "Do not at all concern yourself with a bad illness, unless you are called foolish people."
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.
The first chapter is about cauterizing with fire and about medical cautery, and it is the chapter ordered from head to toe; and about the forms of instruments and the handles of the cauterizer, and about everything that is necessary in the work.
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and about poultices and wounds, and about extracting splinters, and similar things; and it is the chapter ordered with the forms of instruments.
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and sprains, and similar things; and it is ordered from head to toe, with the forms of instruments.
Therefore, so that we remember the work, it is necessary for me to say what quality of aid and of harm each constitution has.
I say that the quality of aid and harm is the longest and most subtle science, with secret responses; and many wise people have spoken about it and agreed on this point; and they spared themselves from saying more because I was afraid to lengthen out my words.
I say that cauterizing always helps all constitutions where there is matter, except for two: that is to say, the hot constitution with matter, and the dry constitution with matter.
In the hot and dry constitution together with matter, some people said that cauterizing is a great aid; and others said the opposite: that cauterizing is of no use for illnesses that come from heat and dryness.
Because the nature of fire is hot, and should not be cured by heat or dryness itself. And those who said the opposite said that cauterizing makes a great aid for hot and dry illnesses that come into the body of men. Because when you compare the body of a man to its moisture and to the temperature of fire, you find that the body is cold.
And I agree with this saying. For I have found it to be true some time through experimentation.
And in any case, no one should meddle with this except the person who has tried it out and knows through long experience the chapter of cauterizing, and the diversity of human constitutions, and the dispositions and causes of their illnesses, and the remedies and the urgency of the moment; as for other constitutions, we have no fear of them, especially in cold and moist illnesses. And all physicians assembled and agreed on cauterizing.
And know, son, that cauterizing with fire is a secret of the cures, and more excellent virtue than cauterizing with burning medicine.
For fire is simple, and does not come into the other member unless it has been cooked, and it harms no other member if its name is known.
The burning medicines spread from one member to another, and bring grievous and difficult-to-heal illnesses to the affected member, and perhaps even kill the sick person.
The fire, by its excellence and due to the goodness of its substance, does not do those things (harm other members or bring grievous illnesses) unless there is excess.
And I have found this to be true by experiment and through the length of my service and study which I have put into the art, and it is based on the truth of things. And for that reason I excuse myself from the length of this speech, because I do not like to deprive you of the secret that is known as fire, and the quality of its work, how it cures illnesses in the body, just as the philosopher says in demonstrating it; accept it in your thoughts.
And know, son, that they agreed on the time when cauterizing should be done, and chose suitable patients.
And I say that cauterizing is suitable at any time, because the remedies for the harm are placed under the aid of cauterizing; and indeed if the cauterizations are for necessary and urgent pains that cannot wait for a more serious illness to come to the member.
And never think, dear son, as foolish doctors do; for the illness that is cured by cauterizing does not have a relapse; and they say so themselves. But it's not like they think, because cauterizing is only medicine that changes the complexion, dries out the humors that are the cause of the pain, and its value lies in its speed, virtue of its work, and lordship over the patient.
And it's possible that the illness comes back sometimes according to the complexion of the sick person and the location of the illness and its nature; and all the superfluities are gathered in him through the acquisition of rich foods and similar things, unless he is given cauterizations at the place where there is too much superfluity and moisture, such as dens for pain and similar things, and that kind of pain does not return. But this is rare.
And many people don't understand why cauterizing is called the last resort of medicine, and that's true; but they think it's only used when other treatments fail, not recognizing that cauterizing can be effective even when other remedies have failed. Their intention is wrong, and cauterizing is indeed the last resort of medicine because when we administer various cures for any illness and they don't work, then we use cauterizing, and it's worth it. So now it is clear that cauterizing is the last resort of medicine, not as foolish doctors intend.
The ancients said that cauterizing with instruments of gold is more effective than those made of iron, and they only said this because of the quality of gold and its high substance. They also said that the place where the cauterization takes effect does not cause corruption, but it's not true, for I have tested it and found that in some people, it is indeed true; and cauterizing with gold is more effective than iron, just as they said.
And in any case, when you heat an instrument of gold, you don't know when it's hot enough because of the color of the gold, and it cools down right away. And if you heat it too much, it melts; so there are many obstacles to consider.
And because of this, we use instruments made of iron for cauterizing, which we have found suitable for our work.
Now I have ordered this chapter according to its main point from start to finish, so that the way is easier to find.
Of olive cauterizing (or olive oil cauterization).
This olive cauterizing is effective against moisture and coldness on the brain, and there are two types: one that is used to treat a disease called "soda", and another due to many humors that descend from the head to the ears; also for a multitude of dreams and pains in the dens (temples), and pain in the throat, and especially against all diseases caused by coldness such as paralysis, stroke, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this olive cauterizing is that the sick person be purged with a solvent medicine that clears the head, three or four times, as the virtues and age and habituation of the sick person can tolerate. And then redress the head of the sick person.
And then have him sit cross-legged between your hands, and place his hands on the balance. Next, place the root of your palm over the root of his nose, between his two eyes; and let the heat come from your middle fingers, if it bleeds, then apply the olive cauterizing instrument, which is called "olivare", in this form:
And place it on the marked spot with ink, and move your hand down and outwards in a slow circular motion, then lift your hand suddenly, and reward yourself if you see the bone 1 small sol; and if you don't see it, repeat the cauterizing until you see the bone as I told you. After that, take 1 spoonful of wool, and place it in a warm compress with soft cotton inside, and apply it to the spot, and leave it for 3 days; after that, apply a moistened cotton ball in butter, and leave it on until the heat is gone; then cure the wound with the ointment called "tetrafarmacion" until it is healed.
And the ancient ones said that the longer the wound remains open, the more it purges, and because of this, it is a greater aid.
And some of them said that you should cauterize so much that part of the bone is burned, and then resorb what has been burned from the bone, and it will be healed.
And others say that you should cauterize so much on top and press it so hard that the bone is lowered as wide as 1 vertole (a type of strip) that women put in their hair, because the humors are thus expelled; and the wound should be left open for a long time; after that, it should be cured and closed.
And I do not think that these two ways of cauterizing are good in all cases, except for those who have a way out through fear; and it seems to me that I should leave them alone. For the eyes are weakened by the continuous solutions of nature, as we observe in other members; and similarly, when the eyes of the sick person are weak by nature, and the first appearance of cauterizing is more salutary and effective for me, and with this method I have been working until now to assemble salvation.
Of the ancient pain of the head.
When the head suffers from chronic pain and lasts for a long time, the sick person should use herbs and pills called "cochie", and purging of the head and oils and plasters. And if they are given the cauterizing that we have spoken of before, it is not effective.
Take note if the eyes of the sick person are naturally strong and not too cold; then cauterize under that with a small amount of what we said before.
Then make cauterizing on each horn of the head up to the bone, as we said before, and make one cauterization at the spot called "vasselz"; it is a place where one puts venesections. And hold your hand so that you don't see up to the bone: the sick person feels a much greater pain in this cauterization than in others, and I will speak of it later; and it is necessary that the cauterizations of the horns be more subtle than those for the middle part of the head. This is the form.
Of the cauterizing of the head. When in some part of the head comes sudden pain, and the disease is spread up to the eye, and the skull is purged by medicinal means and one has done all the other treatments that I have said in the division of diseases, and none of this helps: then make fire cauterization by two methods, namely with a cold remedy and with heat.
The fire cauterization of cold remedy is that you take a lump of alum, and remove the rind and recast it into one vessel as two eyes, and then make an incision in the temple where the pain lies in such a way that you can put the lump of alum under the skin; and when it is replaced, bind and tighten it well and leave it so for twelve hours; and then unbind and treat further the lump of alum, and leave the wound so for two or three days; then put on a cotton poultice in butter until pus comes out.
After the ointment cure as long as it is healed; and if you see that make those from some burning medicines that I have written or secondly to treat burning medicines.

two hairs which prick the eye Sometimes it happens that the hairs of the eyelids become converted into thorns, and prick the eye, and then one makes a cauterization according to two manners: that is to say by fire, or by burning medicine.

When you cure a fistula as we have said in the division of diseases, and your treatments do not benefit at all, then it is necessary that you make a fire cautery according to this manner.
Command the patient to put their head into your hand; and have a servant hold their head between your hands in such a way that it does not move or turn its head. After this, place damp cotton on top of an egg yolk or persimmon juice over the eye; after that heat up the cautery until it is like the shape of a hen's feather, along with the handle being the cautery itself. If you wish, make it pointed from one end to the other, and if you wish, also as wide as a tent; but however, what is shaped in this way will be worth your work.
After press the fistula if it is open, and remove the dirt, and dry it; and if it is closed, work on it, and remove the pus from it. After that place the hot cautery shape over it, and hold your hand with the cautery at the part near the eye so that your hands do not make a mistake; and put the cautery into the fat of the eye, and corrupt it.
If you come to the beginning of the cautery just up to the bone, it would be good. If not, then restart the cautery again once more, and if necessary, and let it stay that way for 3 days; after which put damp cotton on a balm, and the cure of drying ointment until it is healed, and if in 40 days it has not healed, then put on corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered; and the king (of the eye) according to what it would come out in its usual way.

of the abscess of the throat
When an abscess comes in the root of the throat or in the tonsils or on the palate or at the roots of the teeth, after it has made a hole and has become swollen and turned into a fistula; and you treat it as we have said in sections, and it does not help: then it is necessary that you have one cauterizing iron according to the size of the fistula; afterwards put the bottom or poultice of the fistula, and hold your hand so that the iron becomes hot at the point of it, and on the last place; and do this once or twice, afterwards treat it with what we have said in cures, until it is healed; and if it is cured thus, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place and remove the corrupted bone, according to what you will find in its chapter.
of the disease of the teeth (likely referring to dental abscess or tooth decay)
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth move, and you treat it with medicines and they don't work, then put the patient's head in your healthy one; afterwards heat up the cauterizing iron whose form will come forth like this: place a cane on the tooth; and put hot iron inside immediately, and hold your hand so that the patient feels the heat coming to the root of the tooth; afterwards lift your hand and reheat the cauterizing iron according to your will; afterwards fill the patient's mouth with salty water, and let them spit it out once an hour for a long time.
And in this way the tooth that was moving is consolidated, and the softened gums are tightened, and the moisture that had corrupted is dried up.
of the pain of the teeth (likely referring to toothache)
When tooth pain comes from cold or there is cold or warmth inside, and the remedies of medicine don't work, then it's necessary to cauterize in two ways: with butter cauterization and fire cauterization.
Butter cauterization is done in this way: you take a piece of goat butter and shape it into a iron spoon or a bronze one; afterwards take cotton and wrap it around the tip of a tent, and melt it in hot butter, and put it on the painful tooth immediately and hold it until it cools down; and do so often until its virtues come to the root of the tooth. And if you see that the wool and cotton are wet with cold butter, put them on the painful tooth, and then place a hot iron until the fire reaches the apex of the tooth.
The fire cauterization together is done in this way: you take an iron or bronze stick, and it should be thick so that the fire does not reach the mouth of the patient. Then heat up the cauterizing iron, whose shape will come forth like this: and place it on the tooth and hold your hand until the cauterizing iron cools down; do this often, because the pain will be removed that very day or the next.

from swelling

From boils which come to the feet
The boils that come to the feet often bring a disease called "cloves" according to our intention, and it is a large foul thing that hurts the foot. And there are two types of ulcers for this disease. It's either fire ulcer, or hot water ulcer. Fire ulcer is when you heat up one cauterizer like a blacksmith's pen, made of good iron to the size that can pierce the nail from all sides, and have subtle edges; then put it very hot on the clot, and turn your hand completely around the clot until it reaches the core of the clot; and let it be thus for three days until it starts to ooze pus; then make a poultice of bitter wild herbs crushed with oil, and leave the poultice on all night, and the clot will fall off and be pulled out from its roots. Then cure the place with ointment melting fat as long as it is healed.

In this way do another one after another until you have extracted all those that are on the body, then cure the places with ointments that melt fat.

We have told you in the first chapter all disease wherein it is necessary to cauterize with fire, or hot medicine, and the reasons and causes and instruments and forms of cauteries; and I put that chapter at the head until now, and I shall go into this chapter by the same way so that what you want to find will be more easily found.
And so that I begin this, it is necessary, dear son, that you learn that in this chapter there is more danger and fear than in the first of cauteries, and therefore it is necessary to be more careful.
Because in the work of this chapter there are many times evacuation of blood when opening the vein, and to the incision above an abscess, and to the care of wounds, and to the extraction of arrows, and to the incision above a bone, and similar things; or there is doubt and fear, and death comes to many.
And I warn you that you do not take folly into account. For many ways of people come to cures. ...et qui se met sur l'œil. (And from the flesh which grows on the eyelid)
Il est connu qu'à certains hommes une fois apparaît un charroux (une forme de cicatrice) rouge sur la paupière qui recouvre les papiers et se déforme au-dessus, ressemblant à une rose de balauste. Et le traitement consiste à faire que le malade mette son chef en ta main, puis tu ouvres les yeux, et tu attaches toute cette charroux avec des suture convenables à cela, et tu la soutiens à la force ; puis tu coupes la première char, et tu vas tout couper au rasoir jusqu'à la fin, et prends garde de ne pas blessé l'œil par l'opération. Et soit l'incision du spatumile que nous avons dit dans l'incision de l'ungule et les petites forces de quoi on ôte le sebel, comme la forme après ce chapitre en démontre.
Et quant la cicatrice (incision) est accomplie et que toute la charroux (tissu) soit ôtée, alors tu emploies du seil broiet (linon mouillé) ou tu distilles de la coliere rouge (un mélange médicinal corrosif) dans l'œil, et tu mets sur l'œil du coton avec abin d'œuf (une substance utilisée pour tamponner), si cela est assuré d'être une épistème chaude (une infection ou une éruption). Et s'il ne reste rien et que le saignement cesse, alors tu doutes que l'épistème soit chaude, alors laisse l'œil et cure l'épistème de choses apaisant l'épistème chaude ; puis reviens à ton opération (traitement) jusqu'à ce qu'il soit guéri, et ainsi feras-tu avec tous les chars qui sont ajoutés dans l'œil par cette semblance.
Mais il est toujours nécessaire d'observer la voie naturelle pour traiter chaque œil faible, et de ne pas recourir à des opérations de force (ovraige de fer) sauf en cas d'accident. Of the back of the eye
This disease called "alcunnati" is nothing but the back of the eye that gathers in the eye, resembling a thread descending, and it's not a thread; and the way to recognize the treatment is as follows: you make the patient sit on a well-made and straight chair, then take their head between your hands from both sides, and pull until you see the back of the eye come up to your eyes; then be careful that it doesn't descend and be dislodged by light, and look at the things in front of them as they usually do. And if it doesn't descend, know that it's a thread, and if the descending movement of the back of the eye is not made in the way we said, make the patient sit between your hands, then take the fine needle and sew on the corneal membrane one thread by the continuity of the conjunctive with the corneal membrane and the crown, until the back of the eye comes out; and when it has come out, distill in the eye hot water mixed with honey, or onion juice boiled with honey, then be treated with another treatment until it is healed.
From the threads of the eye (i.e., from cataracts)
We have the threads of the eye in their divisions, and what needs to be pierced; do it according to health. It is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands crossed, away from the light of the sun, and with one hand hold the healthy eye tightly closed; then place the paper of the eye in your left hand if the eye is on the left side, or in your right hand if the eye is on the right side. Then take in your right hand, if the eye is on the left side, the instrument called "almagda", or with your left hand, if the eye is on the right side; then place the point of the instrument near the crown at the thickness of a thread in the white part of the eye to the edge of the smaller eyelid. Then push the instrument called "almagda" forcefully and turn your hand until it pierces in the white part of the eye, and feel that almagda comes to the empty thing; and it is necessary that the measure of the way of almagda be at the bottom of the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and this is the crown of the eye. If you see the bone in that paper due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then place the instrument just up to where the thread is, and press it under a way after another. If the thread now descends, then see the patient in front of him and the instrument in his eye.
After a little rest, and if the thread ascends again through the second way, place the forceps with the instrument, and when it is well positioned and does not ascend, carefully extract almagda by turning your hand slowly and slowly. Then reinsert in the eye a little of silk thread, and remove from the inside of the eye.

And then go out of the house slowly and carefully, and change back into your clothes; and know that no disciples should interfere in this case.

It is necessary when you cure the tooth of its pain that you wait as long as possible before pulling it, because this should not be done hastily; for it is a very high substance, and one must have no excuse to pull it. From the blood of the throat

The manner of the fumigation is that you take one pot where there are glowing coals, and let the cover be covered with a perforated cloth in the middle, and on those holes put the instrument; then throw your fumigation into it, and the patient puts the end of the rope in his mouth, and closes his mouth so that no smoke comes out, but rather goes up to the blood; and now the blood will flow, and if it doesn't flow, then repeat the fumigation many times. The chapters of abscesses
There are many forms of abscesses, as can be seen from the memory that comes later in this book, and they differ in perforation and size; and there are two ways in which one is itself, and due to moisture. And the second way is because of the place where it is on the body. For the abscess of the head is different from that of the fundament, and the abscess of the fatty part is different from those that come in joints, and each has its own judgment in its work.
And if it is an abscess that should not be pierced until the swelling is dead according to its completion; and there are those such as it is necessary to pierce when they are thick, and do not die according to their completion, like an abscess that comes near joints. For when an abscess comes near a joint, and one leaves it so long that it becomes rotten around it, by chance the ligament and nerve of the joint become corrupted, causing contraction in that member. Or the abscess is near the principal member, and if you delay perforation until it is dead, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the fundament, then if one pierces it while it is still fresh; and it is not necessary to pierce an abscess that has become thick without being digested, unless it is that which is near the fundament, so that it does not become rotten inside and be prolonged, and come to fistula; and it cannot heal in a long time.
And you should know the work of piercing an abscess that has become dead according to its completion, and it is when the pain has subsided, and the departure of fever, and the diminution of pain and redness, and trembling and hardening of the head of the abscess, and the other signs; and it is necessary to make the perforation in a place more dependent on the abscess, where it opens most easily; and the length of the perforation should be according to the body, and if the abscess is in the hands or feet, or in joints and nerves and arteries, and in all places where there is no pleurisy.
In these swellings, pierce the affected member. And when an abscess is located in a fatty place, it is worth noting that you should leave the perforation until it is fully digested and confirmed; as we have said. For if you puncture before this, you will cause the body to become infected with venom from there, and be full of foul matter; and by chance harden the lips and the deepest part of it. And for any abscess that are pierced according to the width of the body, according to what is needed by the member; and you must bring small abscesses to small punctures; and in large abscesses, large punctures, and many slits according to the similarity of the size of the abscess. And for those abscesses where it is necessary to cut out the core, and cover; when it has become like a cloth, and is softened, then it is worth noting that you should do as one does with infants: and for those that are slit into three angles, and for those that have the core covered like a myrtle leaf, as abscesses of the kidneys; and for those where one makes a round cut, and other cuts and similar coverings.
Those that do not have a head, as soft superficial abscesses, it is necessary to pierce them completely. And when the abscess is large and has thickened with a great tumor, and you pierce it, do not draw out all the tumor at once, but take out part of it; then stop the abscess until the next day, then take out nothing more from there; do this several times in turns, as long as the tumor comes out entirely. Especially if the patient is weak or a woman carrying, or small children, or old men decrepit. For the spirit may shake at some point with the exit of the tumor, that by chance kill the patient, and you will not be aware of it. Therefore, avoid this chapter with great caution, and when you have pierced your abscess, flush the wound, and look if the wound from the abscess is small; and if it's a plain cut, bring linen or old cotton tents; and if it's a large abscess, and there are many punctures, it is necessary that you tie one tent to each other; and if you have removed part of the skin, and wrapped around it, it is necessary that you fill it with old cotton or rags of linen threads moistened, and bind until the third day; then remove and cure with ointment as long as it is healed.
And if the wound becomes inflamed with blood, bring cold and bitter vinegar, and a damp cloth, and apply it to the place where the wound is bleeding several times. And if the inflammation of the wound persists, bring governance and care, and the powders that I said in this book are good for hot wounds, and if it's in summer, and the place or the abscess is very swollen, it is necessary to put on wet tents and drapes in wine and hot oil; and if it's in winter, and the place is fleshy, it is necessary to put on wet drapes in vinegar and oil, or in cold wine and oil, until the third day, as we have said; and then untie the abscess and flush and cure it, as it is necessary, until it is healed.

Erraticque seems to be an archaic or obsolete term. After some research, I found that "erraticque" could be related to the concept of "erratica", which refers to a type of skin condition characterized by irregular or uneven lesions on the skin.
Erratic condition is when the nature of a woman's body is not apparent, or if her breasts are small; and it occurs by birth or by accident from illness that comes to her; or it is because of charlatanry, or subtle or dense bad luck, and it is done inside the womb, or on the sides, or underneath, no matter what; and it blocks conception, childbirth, and possibly causes her to have flowers. The knowledge of this is by looking at the belly. If the protrusions are close to being apparent, you should look with your fingers or a spatula.

The things by which wounds are made are by falling on a stone, and the stroke of a sword or knife, or a lance or club or arrow, or similar things.
or that are made in accordance with the place of the body, such as a wound that bleeds on the neck, or on the head, or on the chest, or on the belly, or on the liver, and similar members.
and I will tell you the treatment of each wound, and I will begin with the wounds of the head, which are simple, because the memory will come to its chapter.
I say that when a simple wound comes to the head and the bone is not broken, be careful if it was caused by falling on a stone or similar things, and the skin is completely torn and the wound is large. and you doubt the formation of a hot apostème: if it happens at once to heal with cephalic liquid itself if it comes out pus from the wound, and the healing is on the contrary side of the damaged part.
and have the patient replenish themselves with wine and large amounts of meat.
And if it doesn't heal, then reduce the amount of food and have them not drink wine or eat meat.
and place on the wound cotton soaked in rose oil or simply in wine stiptic.
and if there is no hot apostème, then place on the wound the powder I will speak of later, when it's fresh with all its blood and not altered by air or moisture.
and if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
and if the wound is large like a cut from a sword and similar things, and the lips of the wound cannot be closed by themselves, then close them with sutures, just as I will say for belly wounds, with stitching.
and if the wound is exposed to the bone and raw, and only held together by a little flesh, then scrape it away; then treat the wound with medicine, and replace hard meat in place of skin. and command the patient to eat large amounts of meat, such as roasted chestnuts and bread, as well as poultry and similar things.
And if you find a purulent skin infection that does not reach to the bone, scrape out all of it and clean, then treat it.
and if there is an artery in the wound that cannot be stopped with a cauterizer, and it won't stop bleeding with medicine, then tie off the artery, and if you find it loose, tie it with thread. and if that doesn't help, then cauterize it.
And if these wounds are small and simple, then the treatment is light; and it is that you put in this powder which heals even when the air has altered it.
And the manner of making the powder is: take equal parts of frankincense, dragon's blood, and quicksilver, and grind them finely. and apply the powder to the wound, and bind it firmly, until it dries well and joins together all its blood.
and then tie it tightly, and leave it without untying, if it swells; for one does not remove the medicine until it is healed.
And sometimes you do this with quicksilver alone, when you don't have frankincense or dragon's blood, but the air has already altered the wound.
And if the air has already changed the wound, even to an ulcer, then don't put this powder in it.
but apply some digestive ointments, or poultices made from barley flour cooked in water and honey, until it discharges pus. then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
And if the break in the bone comes with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with delicate forceps.

and see here the manner of ancient plasters for restoring breaks, dislocations, and twists: and it is that you take flour that remains at the walls of the mill, and mix it with abundant egg, and put this concoction, not too clear nor too thick, in use, and it is the common manner of the plaster restoring the break in many conditions, and also for women and small children, because it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Another manner of plaster restoring the break and dislocation and twist: take 3 aloes, 3 ladani, 2 enule, 2 asefetide, and gum arabic, and mastic, each 10 parts; myrrh and aloes, each 5 parts; and alchil, 8 parts. And make powder of them, and mix with abundant egg; if the complexion of the body is hot, use this plaster, because it has a good composition, and restores the broken bones quickly, and is worth to many men.
Another method for applying ointment that is good for torsion: take mugwort and wormwood, each 20 parts; myrrh and aloes, each 28 parts; acaica 6 parts; Armenian balm 10 parts; crush well, mix with vinegar or the white of an egg, and administer.
Another method for applying ointment to joints and bones that have come out of their place, and it soothes the pain occurring, and the broken bone: take new linen, moisten it with ashes and cooked oil, and put it on the place. This plaster does not heal, but it is good for soothing hot swelling, and properly removing pains.
Another method for applying ointment that heals broken bones: take dried leaves of fig tree and earth pomegranate leaves, and crush them together, and make a plaster.
Another method for applying ointment to restore the breaking of bone and to reduce the remaining swelling: take root of wormwood, chamomile, flowers of violets, flour from wheat, each one part; and crush them, and mix with wine, and bind hot to the affected member, and bind it with moist coriander water or vinegar, and use.
Another method for applying ointment that is stronger than the first one, when hard swelling comes to restore the member. Take roots of wormwood, and seeds of flax, balm, melilotus, and valerian, and flowers of violets and chamomile, each one part; and crush them, and mix with salt water or sweet vinegar or wine, according to the heat of the member and its rest.
In his book, Hippocrates writes that one should not apply anything on a broken limb to restore it stronger than beeswax made with wax and oil, and nothing else more, saying that it should be between two extremes: neither too soft nor too hard.
And it seems to Galen that one should apply on the broken limb to restore it, things that are dry but a little warm, such as resin and aloes and incense and similar things, as we have said.
Another method for applying ointment that is good for lesions and pain: take mugwort and hazelnuts from unpeeled almonds or quail feathers, and wormwood, and equal parts of them, and crush them, and make a plaster with it and the same amount.
And then the swelling will subside a little afterwards, and be careful that the sick person does not feel any pain, nor has the bone moved from its place; if so, do not remove the plaster for 9 days.
And if pain comes to the place, or itching, sense of restlessness, or inflammation, then hasten and remove it, and take away the plaster now; and take a light cloth, and a moist sea sponge, and plunge them in water, and lift up the swelling, until the itching ceases, and the pain is soothed. Then let the member rest for an hour, and then put on linen that is dry but moistened in water or rose oil, and tie it up with a bandage until you are sure of hot swelling.
And let the inflammation of the member rest, and let the pain be removed, then resume the gentle binding, and a little plaster, and do not strain it as hard as before; and bind so gently until it is healed.
And if you see that the hot swelling and redness, pain, and inflammation are completely gone, and you need to apply plaster and binding again, then restart, just as you did initially.
And if none of what we said comes to the member, do not remove the binding until 3 days or 4 or 5 or 7; and at no time let it rest for 20 days, as much as is seen, until the breakage is healed, and approaches the approximation of the flesh above.
Then the swelling will be more pronounced than at first, and the flesh will engorge, just as I said before. And if you see that the swelling is drying out too much, know that inflammation will not come to it. Then arise the bruise with hot water daily, every time you remove it, and let it be each 3rd day, and rinse a little the binding.
By this work therefore the swelling is cut off from the member, and hasten to mend what foolish people do, who restore the broken limb wrongly: it's evil, and it's a fearful work, and I see nothing else but cutting loose and working properly. Of the breaking of the bones of the foot.

Here begins the book of Dislocations (or Fractures)
A dislocation is a displacement of some joint from its place, because it cannot move, and the member is badly shaped, and the patient feels great pain and great injury.
When a dislocation comes to someone, it is necessary to reduce it immediately.
For if one delays, the joint becomes inflamed, and perhaps it is set into place with pain in the joint. Therefore, one should not move it to its normal place while it is inflamed: many times this brings on spasms and other pains.
However, when this happens, one makes the patient rest until the swelling has gone down; then they soak the member in hot water and oil, then gently rub it with a linen cloth, and treat each joint as its cure comes into place.
And I have ordered the chapters of Dislocations from the top of the head to the feet.
of the jawbones (or mandibles)
Something happens when the jawbones are dislocated downwards, and the dislocation of both jaws is in two ways: or they are slightly displaced from their place, and softened a little; or they are completely dislocated, and hang towards the breast, and cut off the saliva from the mouth that it can't hold, nor close its mouth, and enclose its tongue with the words.
When the dislocation is small, it returns by itself in a few dispositions (or without much trouble).
And if it is complete and accomplished, you must rub it quickly, and not delay.
and in such a way that the ministrant (or treating physician) holds the patient's head, and inserts the mirror's soft pouch from one of his hands into the root of the jawbone's dislocation inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on both sides; and with the other two hands, he should support it equally; and command the patient to soften and move their jaws in all directions.
And the ministrant makes it even, so that it returns to its proper place.
And if the reduction is difficult, or even if it's both jawbones together, you should soak them in hot water and oil, until they are softened slightly; nor delay the reduction, as we have said.
And when they are reduced equally and the patient's mouth closes well without difficulty, then put a moist cloth on it made from rose oil and wax, then tie it lightly with soft ligatures.
and let the patient sleep on their back, and their jaws should be supported between two pillows, so that they do not move to either side, nor try to open them, but rather take soft food, until the pain is gone and the jawbones are healed.
and then gradually eat whatever they want lightly, and do not exert themselves to open their mouth to eat or drink, until the jawbones are well healed and sound.
And if the two jawbones cannot be returned to their place within an hour, and are not converted back to their usual state, then fever and severe pain ensue, and perhaps belly cramps, and possibly vomiting of cold, clear fluid.
And when you see this, then know that it is a mortal condition; and many times the person who experiences it dies within eight days.
of the elbow (or forelle)
The elbow cannot be dislocated from the side towards the spine in its continuation, and it is always dislocated to the outside, and one can feel it well.
And the reduction is that the patient lies down on their back and stretches out their arms; then presses the dislocated joint firmly with your palm, and it will return to its place; then put on a poultice and bandage, and tighten it.
and the joint that is towards the shoulder cannot be dislocated; if it is not, and if it does become dislocated, then you should reduce it to its place, as we have said; then put on a poultice and bandage, and tighten it, and command the patient to rest until they are healed.
and similarly, the shoulder joint is reduced when it is dislocated from its place.
of the Dislocation of the Shoulder (or Spaulle)
Know that the shoulder is dislocated only in three ways: first, it dislocates towards the armpit below; second, it dislocates towards the spine; and possibly it dislocates upwards, which happens rarely.
and it does not dislocate backwards for the shoulder, nor does it dislocate downwards for the nerves; and more often it dislocates below towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, for theirs comes out quickly; in those who have enough flesh, it goes back with pain, and returns with pain.
And perhaps it happens to some men that they are percussed or crushed, and the shoulder becomes inflamed with a hot swelling, and they think that it is dislocated because of this.
Then you must examine it carefully, until you are certain; and then begin the treatment.
And it is known that a dislocation occurs when it is downwards towards the armpit, and you will find a clear distinction between the sick shoulder and the healthy one. and you will find at the top of the shoulder a hollow, and under the armpit on the other side, the top of the shoulder, just like an empty space; and the patient cannot return their hand to its normal place, nor move it with any movement.
and also, if it is dislocated upwards, it is not hidden, because you can see it clearly on the outside.
and this manner of dislocation is not difficult to reduce when it is new or if the infant is sick.
And the method is that the ministrant lifts the patient's hand up high, then puts their thumbs under the armpit, and lifts the joint with force up to its place; and the ministrant lifts the hand and extends it upwards, then places it back in; and it will come back quickly.
And if it does not come back as we have said after several days, then the patient should be bathed in hot water; and the ministrant should apply a soothing and calming poultice, such as a decoction of althea, fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and have them administered.
Then have the patient lie on their back, and put a linen cloth of equal thickness, neither too soft nor too hard, under the armpit, then place the ministrant's heel on top of it, and lift the shoulder joint virtuously, and draw the patient's hand.
and another ministrant holds the patient's head, so that it does not move down; and the shoulder returns immediately, and if you see that it reduces in this manner, then...
and that you have present a man who is larger than the patient, and stands directly beside them, and puts their shoulder under the armpit of the patient, and lifts the armpit up high, so that the patient hangs in it. and another ministrant pulls the patient's hand down towards their stomach. And if the patient is too light, they can hold with them something heavy, and the dislocation will return immediately.
And it is also reduced in another manner: and that one puts a long wooden skewer into the ground, whose length is as long as a fist, and neither too thick nor too thin. and it is placed under the patient, but with a soft cloth over it, and the patient's hand is extended downwards on the other side forcefully, and the joint will return to its place quickly.
and if it does not return through all the things that we have said, then do this cure: take a skewer of two arms in length and four fingers in width, whose head is rounded to fit easily into the depth under the armpit, in this shape...
then tie a cloth over the top of the skewer so that the skewer does not hurt the patient, then place it under the armpit of the patient; and extend your arm over the length of the skewer, and tie the elbow on top of the skewer, and the arm at the hand, then place the arm over a wooden splint of one and a half fingers in width, and have the hands extended downwards, and let the body hang from the other side: the joint will return now.
And when it is in place, however it may be, then you should put under the armpit a linen ball of equal size, then apply an ointment made with flour powder, incense and egg fat, over the entire shoulder and around it; then tighten the bandage under the armpit gently, and turn the cloth over the ointment, and bind the hand to the neck, and let the hands remain in this way.
And it is necessary that he eat lightly, until the members are comforted, for it will heal more quickly; and then eat again in 5 or 7 days, and move him; and if it is firm and has not softened, it means it is sound.
And if the joint is not yet in its proper place, or because of moisture that it has come to, or for another reason, then you should administer cauterization at three small punctures, as if they were placed before a cauterizer's needle.
And if you do all of this and after 7 days, the joint still does not hold and cannot lift their arm up, then know that the nerves at the top of the shoulder are cut or stretched or softened; and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The dislocation that occurs towards the spine, and shoulders, or back, is caused by a blow, and stretching of the hands, and it administers another type of injury; and treat until it is healed.
And if after healing comes stiffness in the member and delay in movement, then have the patient bathe many times until the stiffness is alleviated and returns to its original nature.
of the Dislocation of the Shoulder (or Coute)
Know that the joint of the shoulder is dislocated in parts, and returns to its place in parts; and it is dislocated from all sides, and also before and behind, and the dislocation is not easily reduced, because one can see it well and feel it well; and it occurs according to each figure. And when you bring the sick shoulder together with the healthy one, you can see it clearly apart. For the joint is swollen, and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its own shoulder at all.
And it is necessary that you reduce it in such a way that hot swellings come, because if hot swellings come, it will be more painful to reduce; and perhaps it cannot be fully reduced at all, especially when it occurs behind; then it is worse than other dislocations, and is of greater pain, and perhaps death ensues.
And the reduction is such that when it can be reduced, you should extend their hand with your two hands; and their arms should be stretched out, and the ministrant's hands around the shoulder joint, on top of it and underneath, and push the joint into its own place, or to the root of their palm, until it returns to its proper place.

and when it is back in place, they should put on dry ointment with egg fat, and the bandage firmly, and bind the arm to the neck of the patient, and let it be for some time, then release it.
And if the joint is firm in its place, loosen the ligament and leave it; and if you see that the joint does not hold well, reapply the poultice and the ligament, and leave it thus for some days, until it is firmly set; then loosen it.
And if he feels grief after the dislocated joint is reset, and it swells, then bathe him and rub gently, and press until it is eased; or put a thick sheep's fat poultice on the joint, then leave it for one day and night, then remove it, and have him enter the bath; and when he sweats, rub the joint temperately, then put the poultice back on, and then have him enter the bath; and do this twice or three times, until it is healed; and if you see that it does not come back to its place, then put moist warm wadding of marsh grass on it, and bind it tight; it will return to its place.
the dislocation of the hand
The joint of the hand is dislocated, and its return to place is slighter than that of other joints Nevertheless, it must be put back in place hastily, in the same manner as it was dislocated, even if the place swells or becomes inflamed with a hot aposteme.

However, you must make sure that it is properly in place; then have the physician look at it again; then put the ailing hand on its back over a table for stretching and putting back into place. And if the dislocation is in the back of the hand, then have the ailing person put their back on the table, and have the physician put his hand over the appearance of the joint; if it goes back into place now, it's good.

the dislocation of the wrist
At times they are dislocated in front of the hand on both sides, at other times in back of the palm or towards the belly.

from the dislocation of the spine (or more specifically, the vertebrae) at the back.
When the vertebra of the back or neck is dislocated, and there are several that are dislocated, then there is no treatment, for death hastens the ailing person.

And often the vertebra moves to four parts; that which moves in the backside is called "gyphosis".
The treatment is that you correct it if the gyphosis has come from childhood: if there's no correction or healing at all. (Note: Gyphosis, also known as lordosis, is a medical condition where the spine curves abnormally in an inward direction, often affecting the lower back.)

of the thigh dislocations.
It is known that the joints of the thigh and the shoulders do not dislocate other than in two parts, solely, and it does not come from them as from the other joints; and the joint of the thigh is dislocated in four parts: inward and outward, and front and back; and even more so, and frequently dislocated in front and behind.
And the knowledge of its dislocation inward is that when one joins the sick leg with the healthy one, it is longer, and the ailing person's knee opens more than the healthy one's, and the ailing person cannot double their knee to the fold. and it is the spot that is beside the inflamed and hot swelling, because the tendons of the knee come from there.

And the signs of one who it comes out in front, are that his leg is well straightened according to the completion; however, he does not double his knee without pain, and if one wants to make him walk, he cannot move forward, and holds back his urine, and swells up his scrotum, and when he walks, he passes over his heel.
And the signs of one who it comes out behind, are that he cannot stretch his knee, nor double it, and that leg is more twisted than the other, and the scrotum is softened, and the tendons of the knee are at the same level as the ribs.
The remedies for this resemblance of dislocation are that you return to the fact that the dislocation is ancient, and it stays in its position, so there is no cure; and it does not seem necessary to have hope. of the knee dislocation.

And the signs are that you command the patient to bend their knee to their thigh, and if it does not straighten at their thigh, know that they are dislocated.
